(November 2012)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Berke also forged an alliance with the Egyptian Mamluks against Hulagu and supported Kublai's rival claimant, Ariqboke.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barthold_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-barthold-84">[80]</a></sup>
</p><p>Hulagu died on 8 February 1264. Berke sought to take advantage and invade Hulagu's realm, but he died along the way, and a few months later Alghu Khan of the Chagatai Khanate died as well. Kublai named Hulagu's son <a href="/wiki/Abaqa_Khan" title="Abaqa Khan">Abaqa</a> as new Ilkhan, and nominated Batu's grandson <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/M%C3%B6ngke_Tem%C3%BCr" title="Möngke Temür">Möngke Temür</a> to lead the Golden Horde. Abaqa sought foreign alliances, such as attempting to form a <a href="/wiki/Franco-Mongol_alliance" title="Franco-Mongol alliance">Franco-Mongol alliance</a> against the Egyptian Mamluks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[81]</a></sup> Ariqboqe surrendered to Kublai at <a href="/wiki/Shangdu" title="Shangdu">Shangdu</a> on 21 August 1264.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span id="Campaigns_of_Kublai_Khan_.281264.E2.80.931294.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Campaigns_of_Kublai_Khan_(1264–1294)">Campaigns of Kublai Khan (1264–1294)</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kublai_Khan%27s_campaigns" title="Kublai Khan's campaigns">Kublai Khan's campaigns</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="843" data-file-width="2520" decoding="async" height="74" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba.jpg/220px-M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba.jpg/330px-M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba.jpg/440px-M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Samurai" title="Samurai">samurai</a> <a href="/wiki/Takezaki_Suenaga" title="Takezaki Suenaga">Suenaga</a> facing Mongol's bomb and Goryeo's arrows. <i><a href="/wiki/M%C5%8Dko_Sh%C5%ABrai_Ekotoba" title="Mōko Shūrai Ekotoba">Mōko Shūrai Ekotoba</a></i> (蒙古襲来絵詞), circa 1293.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mitsui_Sukenaga.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="861" data-file-width="2597" decoding="async" height="73" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Mitsui_Sukenaga.jpg/220px-Mitsui_Sukenaga.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Mitsui_Sukenaga.jpg/330px-Mitsui_Sukenaga.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Mitsui_Sukenaga.jpg/440px-Mitsui_Sukenaga.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mitsui_Sukenaga.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>defeating the Mongol invasion army (left) Samurai Mitsui Sukenaga (right)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tagezaki_Suenaga,Ekotoba5.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2455" data-file-width="3806" decoding="async" height="142" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Tagezaki_Suenaga%2CEkotoba5.jpg/220px-Tagezaki_Suenaga%2CEkotoba5.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Tagezaki_Suenaga%2CEkotoba5.jpg/330px-Tagezaki_Suenaga%2CEkotoba5.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Tagezaki_Suenaga%2CEkotoba5.jpg/440px-Tagezaki_Suenaga%2CEkotoba5.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tagezaki_Suenaga,Ekotoba5.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Samurai Shiraishi clan</div></div></div>
<p>In the south, after <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Xiangyang" title="Battle of Xiangyang">the fall of Xiangyang</a> in 1273, the Mongols sought the final conquest of the Song dynasty in South China. In 1271, Kublai renamed the new Mongol regime in China as the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a> and sought to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sinicize" title="Sinicize">sinicize</a> his image as <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_China" title="Emperor of China">Emperor of China</a> to win the control of the Chinese people. Kublai moved his headquarters to Khanbaliq, the genesis for what later became the modern city of <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a>. His establishment of a capital there was a controversial move to many Mongols who accused him of being too closely tied to <a href="/wiki/Chinese_culture" title="Chinese culture">Chinese culture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Mongols were eventually successful in their campaigns against (Song) China, and the Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_Song" title="Southern Song">Song imperial family</a> surrendered to the Yuan in 1276, making the Mongols the first non-Chinese people to conquer all of China. Kublai used his base to build a powerful empire, creating an academy, offices, trade ports and canals, and sponsoring arts and science. Mongol records list 20,166 public schools created during his reign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-man_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-man-89">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mongol_warrior_of_Genghis_Khan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="430" data-file-width="760" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Mongol_warrior_of_Genghis_Khan.jpg/220px-Mongol_warrior_of_Genghis_Khan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Mongol_warrior_of_Genghis_Khan.jpg/330px-Mongol_warrior_of_Genghis_Khan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Mongol_warrior_of_Genghis_Khan.jpg/440px-Mongol_warrior_of_Genghis_Khan.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mongol_warrior_of_Genghis_Khan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mongol warrior on horseback, preparing a mounted archery shot.</div></div></div>
<p>After achieving actual or nominal dominion over much of Eurasia and successfully conquering China, Kublai pursued further expansion. His <a href="/wiki/First_Mongol_invasion_of_Burma" title="First Mongol invasion of Burma">invasions of Burma</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sakhalin" title="Sakhalin">Sakhalin</a> were costly, and his <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions_of_Vietnam" title="Mongol invasions of Vietnam">attempted invasions</a> of <a href="/wiki/%C4%90%E1%BA%A1i_Vi%E1%BB%87t" title="Đại Việt">Đại Việt</a> (northern Vietnam) and <a href="/wiki/Champa" title="Champa">Champa</a> (southern Vietnam) ended in devastating defeat, but secured vassal statuses of those countries. The Mongol armies were repeatedly beaten in Đại Việt and were crushed at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_B%E1%BA%A1ch_%C4%90%E1%BA%B1ng_(1288)" title="Battle of Bạch Đằng (1288)">Battle of Bạch Đằng (1288)</a>.
</p><p>Nogai and <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ch%C3%BC" title="Köchü">Konchi</a>, the khan of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/White_Horde" title="White Horde">White Horde</a>, established friendly relations with the Yuan dynasty and the Ilkhanate. Political disagreement among contending branches of the family over the office of great khan continued, but the economic and commercial success of the Mongol Empire continued despite the squabbling.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[87]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1274 and again in 1281, Kublai Khan <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions_of_Japan" title="Mongol invasions of Japan">invaded Japan</a> on two separate occasions. However, he was not able to conquer Japan.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Disintegration_into_competing_entities">Disintegration into competing entities</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Division_of_the_Mongol_Empire" title="Division of the Mongol Empire">Division of the Mongol Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fun%C3%A9railles_de_Tchaghata%C3%AF.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="390" data-file-width="399" decoding="async" height="215" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Fun%C3%A9railles_de_Tchaghata%C3%AF.jpeg/220px-Fun%C3%A9railles_de_Tchaghata%C3%AF.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Fun%C3%A9railles_de_Tchaghata%C3%AF.jpeg/330px-Fun%C3%A9railles_de_Tchaghata%C3%AF.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/87/Fun%C3%A9railles_de_Tchaghata%C3%AF.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fun%C3%A9railles_de_Tchaghata%C3%AF.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The funeral of <a href="/wiki/Chagatai_Khan" title="Chagatai Khan">Chagatai Khan</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Major changes occurred in the Mongol Empire in the late 1200s. Kublai Khan, after having conquered all of China and established the Yuan dynasty, died in 1294. He was succeeded by his grandson <a href="/wiki/Tem%C3%BCr_Khan" title="Temür Khan">Temür Khan</a>, who continued Kublai's policies. At the same time the <a href="/wiki/Toluid_Civil_War" title="Toluid Civil War">Toluid Civil War</a>, along with the <a href="/wiki/Berke%E2%80%93Hulagu_war" title="Berke–Hulagu war">Berke–Hulagu war</a> and the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Kaidu%E2%80%93Kublai_war" title="Kaidu–Kublai war">Kaidu–Kublai war</a>, greatly weakened the authority of the great khan over the entirety of the Mongol Empire and the empire fractured into autonomous khanates, the Yuan dynasty and the three western khanates: the Golden Horde, the Chagatai Khanate and the Ilkhanate. Only the Ilkhanate remained loyal to the Yuan court but endured its own power struggle, in part because of a dispute with the growing Islamic factions within the southwestern part of the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-93">[89]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the death of <a href="/wiki/Kaidu" title="Kaidu">Kaidu</a>, the Chatagai ruler Duwa initiated a peace proposal and persuaded the Ögedeids to submit to Temür Khan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[91]</a></sup> In 1304, all of the khanates approved a peace treaty and accepted Yuan emperor Temür's supremacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[94]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[95]</a></sup> This established the nominal supremacy of the Yuan dynasty over the western khanates, which was to last for several decades. This supremacy was based on weaker foundations than that of the earlier Khagans and each of the four khanates continued to develop separately and function as independent states.
</p><p>Nearly a century of conquest and civil war was followed by relative stability, the <i><a href="/wiki/Pax_Mongolica" title="Pax Mongolica">Pax Mongolica</a></i>, and international trade and cultural exchanges flourished between Asia and Europe. Communication between the Yuan dynasty in China and the Ilkhanate in Persia further encouraged trade and commerce between east and west. Patterns of Yuan royal textiles could be found on the opposite side of the empire adorning Armenian decorations; trees and vegetables were transplanted across the empire; and technological innovations spread from Mongol dominions toward the West.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[96]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/Pope_John_XXII" title="Pope John XXII">Pope John XXII</a> was presented a memorandum from the eastern church describing the Pax Mongolica: "... Khagan is one of the greatest monarchs and all lords of the state, e.g., the king of Almaligh (Chagatai Khanate), emperor Abu Said and Uzbek Khan, are his subjects, saluting his holiness to pay their respects."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[97]</a></sup> However, while the four khanates continued to interact with one another well into the 14th century, they did so as <a href="/wiki/Sovereign_state" title="Sovereign state">sovereign states</a> and never again pooled their resources in a cooperative military endeavor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_of_the_khanates">Development of the khanates</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Xian_2006_6-27.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1252" data-file-width="847" decoding="async" height="251" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Xian_2006_6-27.jpg/170px-Xian_2006_6-27.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Xian_2006_6-27.jpg/255px-Xian_2006_6-27.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Xian_2006_6-27.jpg/340px-Xian_2006_6-27.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Xian_2006_6-27.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mongol rider, <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tem%C3%BCr_Chabar_Toqta_%C3%96ldje%C3%AFtu.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1181" data-file-width="1908" decoding="async" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Tem%C3%BCr_Chabar_Toqta_%C3%96ldje%C3%AFtu.jpeg/220px-Tem%C3%BCr_Chabar_Toqta_%C3%96ldje%C3%AFtu.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Tem%C3%BCr_Chabar_Toqta_%C3%96ldje%C3%AFtu.jpeg/330px-Tem%C3%BCr_Chabar_Toqta_%C3%96ldje%C3%AFtu.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Tem%C3%BCr_Chabar_Toqta_%C3%96ldje%C3%AFtu.jpeg/440px-Tem%C3%BCr_Chabar_Toqta_%C3%96ldje%C3%AFtu.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tem%C3%BCr_Chabar_Toqta_%C3%96ldje%C3%AFtu.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A European depiction of the four khans, Temür (Yuan), Chapar (<a href="/wiki/House_of_%C3%96gedei" title="House of Ögedei">House of Ögedei</a>), <a href="/wiki/Toqta" title="Toqta">Toqta</a> (Golden Horde), and Öljaitü (Ilkhanate), in the <i>Fleur des histoires d'orient</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[99]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In spite of his conflicts with Kaidu and Duwa, Yuan emperor Temür established a tributary relationship with the war-like <a href="/wiki/Shan_people" title="Shan people">Shan people</a> after his series of military operations against <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> from 1297 to 1303. This was to mark the end of the southern expansion of the Mongols.
</p><p>When <a href="/wiki/Ghazan" title="Ghazan">Ghazan</a> took the throne of the Ilkhanate in 1295, he formally accepted Islam as his own religion, marking a turning point in Mongol history after which Mongol Persia became more and more Islamic. Despite this, Ghazan continued to strengthen ties with Temür Khan and the Yuan dynasty in the east. It was politically useful to advertise the great khan's authority in the Ilkhanate, because the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Horde" title="Golden Horde">Golden Horde</a> in Russia had long made claims on nearby Georgia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-93">[89]</a></sup> Within four years, Ghazan began sending tribute to the Yuan court and appealing to other khans to accept Temür Khan as their overlord. He oversaw an extensive program of cultural and scientific interaction between the Ilkhanate and the Yuan dynasty in the following decades.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ghazan's faith may have been Islamic, but he continued his ancestors' war with the Egyptian Mamluks, and consulted with his old Mongol advisers in his native tongue. He defeated the Mamluk army at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Wadi_al-Khazandar" title="Battle of Wadi al-Khazandar">Battle of Wadi al-Khazandar</a> in 1299, but he was only briefly able to occupy Syria, due to distracting raids from the <a href="/wiki/Chagatai_Khanate" title="Chagatai Khanate">Chagatai Khanate</a> under its <i>de facto</i> ruler <a href="/wiki/Kaidu" title="Kaidu">Kaidu</a>, who was <a href="/wiki/Kaidu%E2%80%93Kublai_war" title="Kaidu–Kublai war">at war</a> with both the Ilkhans and the Yuan dynasty.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Struggling for influence within the Golden Horde, Kaidu sponsored his own candidate Kobeleg against <a href="/wiki/Bayan_(khan)" title="Bayan (khan)">Bayan</a> (r. 1299–1304), the khan of the White Horde. Bayan, after receiving military support from the Mongols in Russia, requested assistance from both Temür Khan and the Ilkhanate to organize a unified attack against Kaidu's forces. Temür was amenable and attacked Kaidu a year later. After a bloody battle with Temür's armies near the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zawkhan_River" title="Zawkhan River">Zawkhan River</a> in 1301, Kaidu died and was succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Duwa" title="Duwa">Duwa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[102]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MongolsInHungary1241.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="505" data-file-width="741" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/MongolsInHungary1241.jpg/220px-MongolsInHungary1241.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/MongolsInHungary1241.jpg/330px-MongolsInHungary1241.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/MongolsInHungary1241.jpg/440px-MongolsInHungary1241.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MongolsInHungary1241.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Hungarian King <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B%C3%A9la_IV" title="Béla IV">Béla IV</a> in flight from the Mongols under general <a href="/wiki/Kadan" title="Kadan">Kadan</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Horde" title="Golden Horde">Golden Horde</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Duwa was challenged by Kaidu's son Chapar, but with the assistance of Temür, Duwa defeated the Ögedeids. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tokhta" title="Tokhta">Tokhta</a> of the Golden Horde, also seeking a general peace, sent 20,000 men to buttress the Yuan frontier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[103]</a></sup> Tokhta died in 1312, though, and was succeeded by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ozbeg" title="Ozbeg">Ozbeg</a> (r. 1313–41), who seized the throne of the Golden Horde and persecuted non-Muslim Mongols. The Yuan's influence on the Horde was largely reversed and border clashes between Mongol states resumed. <a href="/wiki/Ayurbarwada_Buyantu_Khan" title="Ayurbarwada Buyantu Khan">Ayurbarwada Buyantu Khan</a>'s envoys backed Tokhta's son against Ozbeg.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In the Chagatai Khanate, <a href="/wiki/Esen_Buqa_I" title="Esen Buqa I">Esen Buqa I</a> (r. 1309–1318) was enthroned as khan after suppressing a sudden rebellion by Ögedei's descendants and driving Chapar into exile. The Yuan and Ilkhanid armies eventually attacked the Chagatai Khanate. Recognising the potential economic benefits and the Genghisid legacy, Ozbeg reopened friendly relations with the Yuan in 1326. He strengthened ties with the Muslim world as well, building mosques and other elaborate structures such as baths.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> By the second decade of the 14th century, Mongol invasions had further decreased. In 1323, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_Sa%27id_(Ilkhanid_dynasty)" title="Abu Sa'id (Ilkhanid dynasty)">Abu Said Khan</a> (r. 1316–35) of the Ilkhanate signed a peace treaty with Egypt. At his request, the Yuan court awarded his custodian <a href="/wiki/Chupan" title="Chupan">Chupan</a> the title of commander-in-chief of all Mongol khanates, but Chupan died in late 1327.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[104]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Civil_war" title="Civil war">Civil war</a> erupted in the Yuan dynasty in 1328–29. After the death of Yesün Temür in 1328, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tugh_Tem%C3%BCr" title="Tugh Temür">Tugh Temür</a> became the new leader in Khanbaliq, while Yesün Temür's son <a href="/wiki/Ragibagh_Khan" title="Ragibagh Khan">Ragibagh</a> succeeded to the throne in Shangdu, leading to the civil war known as the <a href="/wiki/War_of_the_Two_Capitals" title="War of the Two Capitals">War of the Two Capitals</a>. Tugh Temür defeated Ragibagh, but the Chagatai khan <a href="/wiki/Eljigidey" title="Eljigidey">Eljigidey</a> (r. 1326–29) supported <a href="/wiki/Khutughtu_Khan_Kusala" title="Khutughtu Khan Kusala">Kusala</a>, elder brother of Tugh Temür, as great khan. He invaded with a commanding force, and Tugh Temür abdicated. Kusala was elected khan on 30 August 1329. Kusala was then poisoned by a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kypchak" title="Kypchak">Kypchak</a> commander under Tugh Temür, who returned to power.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Asia_in_1335.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="837" data-file-width="1100" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Asia_in_1335.svg/220px-Asia_in_1335.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Asia_in_1335.svg/330px-Asia_in_1335.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Asia_in_1335.svg/440px-Asia_in_1335.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Asia_in_1335.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The successor states of the Mongol Empire in 1335: the <a href="/wiki/Ilkhanate" title="Ilkhanate">Ilkhanate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Golden_Horde" title="Golden Horde">Golden Horde</a>, <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chagatai_Khanate" title="Chagatai Khanate">Chagatai Khanate</a></div></div></div>
<p>Tugh Temür (1304–32) was knowledgeable about Chinese language and history and was also a creditable poet, calligrapher, and painter. In order to be accepted by other khanates as the sovereign of the Mongol world, he sent Genghisid princes and descendants of notable Mongol generals to the Chagatai Khanate, Ilkhan Abu Said, and Ozbeg. In response to the emissaries, they all agreed to send tribute each year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[105]</a></sup> Furthermore, Tugh Temür gave lavish presents and an imperial seal to Eljigidey to mollify his anger.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relict_states_of_the_Mongol_Empire">Relict states of the Mongol Empire</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Iron_Helmet,_Mongol_Empire_(19219750434).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3456" data-file-width="5184" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Iron_Helmet%2C_Mongol_Empire_%2819219750434%29.jpg/220px-Iron_Helmet%2C_Mongol_Empire_%2819219750434%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Iron_Helmet%2C_Mongol_Empire_%2819219750434%29.jpg/330px-Iron_Helmet%2C_Mongol_Empire_%2819219750434%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/Iron_Helmet%2C_Mongol_Empire_%2819219750434%29.jpg/440px-Iron_Helmet%2C_Mongol_Empire_%2819219750434%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Iron_Helmet,_Mongol_Empire_(19219750434).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Iron helmet, Mongol Empire</div></div></div>
<p>With the death of Ilkhan <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abu_Sa%27id_(Ilkhanid_dynasty)" title="Abu Sa'id (Ilkhanid dynasty)">Abu Said Bahatur</a> in 1335, Mongol rule faltered and Persia fell into political anarchy. A year later his successor was killed by an Oirat governor, and the Ilkhanate was divided between the Suldus, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jalayir" title="Jalayir">Jalayir</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/J%C3%B6chi_Khasar" title="Jöchi Khasar">Qasarid</a> <a href="/wiki/Togha_Tem%C3%BCr" title="Togha Temür">Togha Temür</a> (d. 1353), and Persian warlords. Taking advantage of the chaos, the <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgians</a> pushed the Mongols out of their territory, and the Uyghur commander <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ghiyath_al-Din_Eretna&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ghiyath al-Din Eretna (page does not exist)">Eretna</a> established an independent state (<a href="/wiki/Eretnids" title="Eretnids">Eretnids</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolia</a> in 1336. Following the downfall of their Mongol masters, the loyal vassal, the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Kingdom_of_Cilicia" title="Armenian Kingdom of Cilicia">Armenian Kingdom of Cilicia</a>, received escalating threats from the Mamluks and were eventually overrun in 1375.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[106]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bitva_na_sinix_vodax.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1357" data-file-width="2310" decoding="async" height="129" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Bitva_na_sinix_vodax.jpg/220px-Bitva_na_sinix_vodax.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Bitva_na_sinix_vodax.jpg/330px-Bitva_na_sinix_vodax.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Bitva_na_sinix_vodax.jpg/440px-Bitva_na_sinix_vodax.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bitva_na_sinix_vodax.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Blue_Waters" title="Battle of Blue Waters">Battle of Blue Waters</a> in 1362, in which <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Lithuania" title="Grand Duchy of Lithuania">Lithuania</a> successfully pushed the Golden Horde from the <a href="/wiki/Principality_of_Kiev" title="Principality of Kiev">Principality of Kiev</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Along with the dissolution of the Ilkhanate in Persia, Mongol rulers in China and the <a href="/wiki/Chagatai_Khanate" title="Chagatai Khanate">Chagatai Khanate</a> were also in turmoil. The plague known as the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a>, which started in the Mongol dominions and spread to Europe, added to the confusion. Disease devastated all the khanates, cutting off commercial ties and killing millions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[107]</a></sup> Plague may have taken 50 million lives in Europe alone in the 14th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[108]</a></sup>
</p><p>As the power of the Mongols declined, chaos erupted throughout the empire as non-Mongol leaders expanded their own influence. The Golden Horde lost all of its western dominions (including modern <a href="/wiki/Belarus" title="Belarus">Belarus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a>) to Poland and <a href="/wiki/Lithuania" title="Lithuania">Lithuania</a> between 1342 and 1369. Muslim and non-Muslim princes in the Chagatai Khanate warred with each other from 1331 to 1343, and the Chagatai Khanate disintegrated when non-Genghisid warlords set up their own puppet khans in <a href="/wiki/Transoxiana" title="Transoxiana">Transoxiana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moghulistan" title="Moghulistan">Moghulistan</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Janibeg" title="Janibeg">Janibeg</a> Khan (r. 1342–1357) briefly reasserted Jochid dominance over the Chaghataids. Demanding submission from an offshoot of the Ilkhanate in <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>, he boasted that "today three uluses are under my control".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prawdin._p._379_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prawdin._p._379-113">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:116px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Menli_I_Giray.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="196" data-file-width="114" decoding="async" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/84/Menli_I_Giray.jpg" width="114"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Menli_I_Giray.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crimean" title="Crimean">Crimean</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tatar" title="Tatar">Tatar</a> <a href="/wiki/Khan_(title)" title="Khan (title)">khan</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mengli_Giray" title="Mengli Giray">Mengli Giray</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>However, rival families of the Jochids began fighting for the throne of the Golden Horde after the assassination of his successor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Berdibek" title="Berdibek">Berdibek</a> Khan in 1359. The last Yuan ruler <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Toghan_Tem%C3%BCr" title="Toghan Temür">Toghan Temür</a> (r. 1333–70) was powerless to regulate those troubles, a sign that the empire had nearly reached its end. His court's unbacked currency had entered a <a href="/wiki/Hyperinflation" title="Hyperinflation">hyperinflationary spiral</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Red_Turban_Rebellion" title="Red Turban Rebellion">the Han-Chinese people revolted</a> due to the Yuan's harsh impositions. In the 1350s, <a href="/wiki/Gongmin_of_Goryeo" title="Gongmin of Goryeo">Gongmin of Goryeo</a> successfully pushed Mongol garrisons back and exterminated the family of Toghan Temür Khan's empress while <a href="/wiki/Tai_Situ_Changchub_Gyaltsen" title="Tai Situ Changchub Gyaltsen">Tai Situ Changchub Gyaltsen</a> managed to eliminate the Mongol influence in Tibet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prawdin._p._379_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-Prawdin._p._379-113">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>Increasingly isolated from their subjects, the Mongols quickly lost most of China to the rebellious Ming forces and in 1368 fled to their heartland in Mongolia. After the overthrow of the Yuan dynasty the Golden Horde lost touch with Mongolia and China, while the two main parts of the Chagatai Khanate were defeated by <a href="/wiki/Timur" title="Timur">Timur</a> (Tamerlane) (1336–1405), who founded the <a href="/wiki/Timurid_Empire" title="Timurid Empire">Timurid Empire</a>. However, remnants of the Chagatai Khanate survived; the last Chagataid state to survive was the <a href="/wiki/Yarkent_Khanate" title="Yarkent Khanate">Yarkent Khanate</a>, until its defeat by the Oirat <a href="/wiki/Dzungar_Khanate" title="Dzungar Khanate">Dzungar Khanate</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Dzungar_conquest_of_Altishahr" title="Dzungar conquest of Altishahr">Dzungar conquest of Altishahr</a> in 1680. The Golden Horde broke into smaller Turkic-hordes that declined steadily in power over four centuries. Among them, the khanate's shadow, the <a href="/wiki/Great_Horde" title="Great Horde">Great Horde</a>, survived until 1502, when one of its successors, the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_Khanate" title="Crimean Khanate">Crimean Khanate</a>, sacked Sarai.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Halperin._p._28_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-Halperin._p._28-114">[110]</a></sup> The Crimean Khanate lasted until 1783, whereas khanates such as the <a href="/wiki/Khanate_of_Bukhara" title="Khanate of Bukhara">Khanate of Bukhara</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kazakh_Khanate" title="Kazakh Khanate">Kazakh Khanate</a> lasted even longer.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_organization">Military organization</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mongol_military_tactics_and_organization" title="Mongol military tactics and organization">Mongol military tactics and organization</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Genghis_Khan_The_Exhibition_(5465078899).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="949" data-file-width="1430" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Genghis_Khan_The_Exhibition_%285465078899%29.jpg/220px-Genghis_Khan_The_Exhibition_%285465078899%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Genghis_Khan_The_Exhibition_%285465078899%29.jpg/330px-Genghis_Khan_The_Exhibition_%285465078899%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Genghis_Khan_The_Exhibition_%285465078899%29.jpg/440px-Genghis_Khan_The_Exhibition_%285465078899%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Genghis_Khan_The_Exhibition_(5465078899).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reconstruction of a Mongol warrior</div></div></div>
<p>The number of troops mustered by the Mongols is the subject of some scholarly debate,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[111]</a></sup> but was at least 105,000 in 1206.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[112]</a></sup> The Mongol military organization was simple but effective, based on the <a href="/wiki/Decimal" title="Decimal">decimal</a> system. The army was built up from squads of ten men each, arbans (10 people), zuuns (100), <a href="/wiki/Mingghan" title="Mingghan">Mingghans</a> (1000), and <a href="/wiki/Tumen_(unit)" title="Tumen (unit)">tumens</a> (10,000).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morgan._pp._80_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan._pp._80-117">[113]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Mongols were most famous for their <a href="/wiki/Mounted_archery" title="Mounted archery">horse archers</a>, but troops armed with lances were equally skilled, and the Mongols recruited other military specialists from the lands they conquered. With experienced <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">Chinese</a> engineers and a bombardier corps which was expert at building <a href="/wiki/Trebuchet" title="Trebuchet">trebuchets</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catapult" title="Catapult">catapults</a> and other machines, the Mongols could lay siege to fortified positions, sometimes building machinery on the spot using available local resources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morgan._pp._80_117-1"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan._pp._80-117">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Subudei.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="900" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="255" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Subudei.jpg/170px-Subudei.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Subudei.jpg/255px-Subudei.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Subudei.jpg/340px-Subudei.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Subudei.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mongol general <a href="/wiki/Subutai" title="Subutai">Subutai</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Horde" title="Golden Horde">Golden Horde</a></div></div></div>
<p>Forces under the command of the Mongol Empire were trained, organized, and equipped for mobility and speed. Mongol soldiers were more lightly armored than many of the armies they faced but were able to make up for it with maneuverability. Each Mongol warrior would usually travel with multiple horses, allowing him to quickly switch to a fresh mount as needed. In addition, soldiers of the Mongol army functioned independently of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Military_supply_chain_management" title="Military supply chain management">supply lines</a>, considerably speeding up army movement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morgan._The_Mongols._pp._74–75_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan._The_Mongols._pp._74–75-118">[114]</a></sup> Skillful use of couriers enabled the leaders of these armies to maintain contact with each other.
</p><p>Discipline was inculcated during a <i>nerge</i> (traditional hunt), as reported by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ata_al-Mulk_Juvayni" title="Ata al-Mulk Juvayni">Juvayni</a>. These hunts were distinctive from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hunt" title="Hunt">hunts</a> in other cultures, being the equivalent to small unit actions. Mongol forces would spread out in a line, surround an entire region, and then drive all of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Game_(food)" title="Game (food)">game</a> within that area together. The goal was to let none of the animals escape and to slaughter them all.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morgan._The_Mongols._pp._74–75_118-1"><a href="#cite_note-Morgan._The_Mongols._pp._74–75-118">[114]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another advantage of the Mongols was their ability to traverse large distances, even in unusually cold winters; for instance, frozen rivers led them like highways to large urban centers on their banks. The Mongols were adept at river-work, crossing the river <a href="/wiki/Saj%C3%B3" title="Sajó">Sajó</a> in spring flood conditions with thirty thousand cavalry soldiers in a single night during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Mohi" title="Battle of Mohi">Battle of Mohi</a> (April 1241) to defeat the Hungarian king <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B%C3%A9la_IV" title="Béla IV">Béla IV</a>. Similarly, in the attack against the Muslim <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muhammad_II_of_Khwarezm" title="Muhammad II of Khwarezm">Khwarezmshah</a> a flotilla of barges was used to prevent escape on the river.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Traditionally known for their prowess with ground forces, the Mongols rarely used <a href="/wiki/Navy" title="Navy">naval</a> power. In the 1260s and 1270s they used seapower while conquering the <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a> of China, though their attempts to mount seaborne <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions_of_Japan" title="Mongol invasions of Japan">campaigns against Japan</a> were unsuccessful. Around the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Mediterranean" title="Eastern Mediterranean">Eastern Mediterranean</a>, their campaigns were almost exclusively land-based, with the seas controlled by the <a href="/wiki/Crusader_states" title="Crusader states">Crusader</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mamluk" title="Mamluk">Mamluk</a> forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>All military campaigns were preceded by careful planning, reconnaissance, and the gathering of sensitive information relating to enemy territories and forces. The success, organization, and mobility of the Mongol armies permitted them to fight on several fronts at once. All adult males up to the age of 60 were eligible for conscription into the army, a source of honor in their tribal warrior tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[116]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society">Society</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Society_of_the_Mongol_Empire" title="Society of the Mongol Empire">Society of the Mongol Empire</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Law_and_governance">Law and governance</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Organization_of_the_Mongol_Empire_under_Genghis_Khan" title="Organization of the Mongol Empire under Genghis Khan">Organization of the Mongol Empire under Genghis Khan</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DiezAlbumsExecution.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1154" data-file-width="1659" decoding="async" height="153" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/DiezAlbumsExecution.jpg/220px-DiezAlbumsExecution.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/DiezAlbumsExecution.jpg/330px-DiezAlbumsExecution.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/DiezAlbumsExecution.jpg/440px-DiezAlbumsExecution.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DiezAlbumsExecution.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The executed – the long and full beard probably means he is not a Mongol – has been thrown off a cliff.</div></div></div>
<p>The Mongol Empire was governed by a code of law devised by Genghis, called <i><a href="/wiki/Yassa" title="Yassa">Yassa</a></i>, meaning "order" or "decree". A particular canon of this code was that those of rank shared much the same hardship as the common man. It also imposed severe penalties, <i>e.g.</i>, the <a href="/wiki/Capital_punishment" title="Capital punishment">death penalty</a> if one mounted soldier following another did not pick up something dropped from the mount in front. Penalties were also decreed for rape and to some extent for murder. Any resistance to Mongol rule was met with massive collective punishment. Cities were destroyed and their inhabitants slaughtered if they defied Mongol orders.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Under <i>Yassa</i>, chiefs and generals were selected based on <a href="/wiki/Meritocracy" title="Meritocracy">merit</a>. The empire was governed by a non-democratic, <a href="/wiki/Parliament" title="Parliament">parliamentary</a>-style central assembly, called <a href="/wiki/Kurultai" title="Kurultai">kurultai</a>, in which the Mongol chiefs met with the great khan to discuss domestic and foreign policies. Kurultais were also convened for the selection of each new great khan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[117]</a></sup>
</p><p>Genghis Khan also created a national seal, encouraged the use of a written alphabet in Mongolia, and exempted teachers, lawyers, and artists from taxes.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The Mongols imported Central Asian Muslims to serve as administrators in China and sent Han Chinese and Khitans from China to serve as administrators over the Muslim population in Bukhara in Central Asia, thus using foreigners to curtail the power of the local peoples of both lands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[118]</a></sup> The Mongols were tolerant of other religions, and rarely persecuted people on religious grounds. This was associated with their culture and progressive thought. Some historians of the 20th century thought this was a good military strategy: when Genghis was at war with Sultan Muhammad of Khwarezm, other Islamic leaders did not join the fight, as it was seen as a non-holy war between two individual powers.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religions">Religions</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_the_Mongol_Empire" title="Religion in the Mongol Empire">Religion in the Mongol Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GhazanConversionToIslam.JPG"><img alt="Painting of a stylized building, showing Ghazan kneeling and accepting conversion" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2009" data-file-width="1498" decoding="async" height="295" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/GhazanConversionToIslam.JPG/220px-GhazanConversionToIslam.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/GhazanConversionToIslam.JPG/330px-GhazanConversionToIslam.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/GhazanConversionToIslam.JPG/440px-GhazanConversionToIslam.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GhazanConversionToIslam.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Persian_miniature" title="Persian miniature">Persian miniature</a> depicting <a href="/wiki/Ghazan" title="Ghazan">Ghazan</a>'s conversion from Buddhism to <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>At the time of Genghis Khan, virtually every religion had found Mongol converts, from <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> to <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Manichaeism" title="Manichaeism">Manichaeism</a> to <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>. To avoid strife, Genghis Khan set up an institution that ensured complete religious freedom, though he himself was a <a href="/wiki/Shamanism" title="Shamanism">shamanist</a>. Under his administration, all religious leaders were exempt from taxation and from public service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[119]</a></sup>
</p><p>Initially there were few formal places of worship because of the nomadic lifestyle. However, under Ögedei (1186–1241), several building projects were undertaken in the Mongol capital. Along with palaces, Ögedei built houses of worship for the Buddhist, Muslim, Christian, and <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">Taoist</a> followers. The dominant religions at that time were <a href="/wiki/Tengrism" title="Tengrism">Tengrism</a> and Buddhism, although Ögedei's wife was a Nestorian Christian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>Eventually, each of the successor states adopted the dominant religion of the local populations: the Mongol-ruled Chinese Yuan dynasty in the East (originally the Great Khan's domain) embraced Buddhism and Shamanism, while the three Western khanates adopted Islam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foltz._pp._105–106_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Foltz._pp._105–106-125">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[122]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[123]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arts_and_literature">Arts and literature</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_historical_cities_and_towns_of_Mongolia" title="List of historical cities and towns of Mongolia">List of historical cities and towns of Mongolia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_literature" title="Mongolian literature">Mongolian literature</a></div>
<p>The oldest surviving literary work in the <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_language" title="Mongolian language">Mongolian language</a> is <i><a href="/wiki/The_Secret_History_of_the_Mongols" title="The Secret History of the Mongols">The Secret History of the Mongols</a></i>, which was written for the royal family some time after Genghis Khan's death in 1227. It is the most significant native account of Genghis's life and genealogy, covering his origins and childhood through to the establishment of the Mongol Empire and the reign of his son, Ögedei.
</p><p>Another classic from the empire is the <i><a href="/wiki/Jami%27_al-tawarikh" title="Jami' al-tawarikh">Jami' al-tawarikh</a></i>, or "Universal History". It was commissioned in the early 14th century by the Ilkhan <a href="/wiki/Abaqa_Khan" title="Abaqa Khan">Abaqa Khan</a> as a way of documenting the entire world's history, to help establish the Mongols' own cultural legacy.
</p><p>Mongol scribes in the 14th century used a mixture of resin and vegetable pigments as a primitive form of <a href="/wiki/Correction_fluid" title="Correction fluid">correction fluid</a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[124]</a></sup> this is arguably its first known usage.
</p><p>The Mongols also appreciated the visual arts, though their taste in portraiture was strictly focused on portraits of their horses, rather than of people.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Science">Science</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SanjufiniZij1.jpg"><img alt="Sanjufini Zij by al-Sanjufini" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="727" data-file-width="1014" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/SanjufiniZij1.jpg/220px-SanjufiniZij1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/SanjufiniZij1.jpg/330px-SanjufiniZij1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/SanjufiniZij1.jpg/440px-SanjufiniZij1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SanjufiniZij1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 1363 astronomical handbook with Middle Mongolian glosses. Known as the Sanjufini Zij.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DiezAlbumsAstronomers.jpg"><img alt="Mongols and Persian astronomers" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1314" data-file-width="2517" decoding="async" height="115" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/DiezAlbumsAstronomers.jpg/220px-DiezAlbumsAstronomers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/DiezAlbumsAstronomers.jpg/330px-DiezAlbumsAstronomers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/DiezAlbumsAstronomers.jpg/440px-DiezAlbumsAstronomers.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DiezAlbumsAstronomers.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mongols look on as Persian astronomers work. Early 14th century illustration in the <a href="/wiki/Jami%27_al-tawarikh" title="Jami' al-tawarikh">Compendium of Chronicles</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The Mongol Empire saw some significant developments in science due to the patronage of the Khans. Roger Bacon attributed the success of the Mongols as world conquerors principally to their devotion to mathematics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[125]</a></sup> Astronomy was one branch of science that the Khans took a personal interest in. According to the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Yuan" title="History of Yuan">Yuanshi</a>, Ögedei Khan twice ordered the armillary sphere of Zhongdu to be repaired (in 1233 and 1236) and also ordered in 1234 the revision and adoption of the Damingli calendar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[126]</a></sup> He built a Confucian temple for Yelü Chucai in Karakorum around 1236 where Yelü Chucai created and regulated a calendar on the Chinese model. <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6ngke_Khan" title="Möngke Khan">Möngke Khan</a> was noted by Rashid al-Din as having solved some of the difficult problems of Euclidean geometry on his own and written to his brother Hulagu Khan to send him the astronomer <a href="/wiki/Nasir_al-Din_al-Tusi" title="Nasir al-Din al-Tusi">Tusi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[127]</a></sup> Möngke Khan's desire to have Tusi build him an observatory in Karakorum did not reach fruition as the Khan died on campaign in southern China. <a href="/wiki/Hulagu_Khan" title="Hulagu Khan">Hulagu Khan</a> instead gave Tusi a grant to build the Maragheh Observatory in Persia in 1259 and ordered him to prepare astronomical tables for him in 12 years, despite Tusi asking for 30 years. Tusi successfully produced the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ilkhanic_Tables" title="Ilkhanic Tables">Ilkhanic Tables</a> in 12 years, produced a revised edition of Euclid's elements and taught the innovative mathematical device called the <a href="/wiki/Tusi_couple" title="Tusi couple">Tusi couple</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Maragheh_observatory" title="Maragheh observatory">Maragheh Observatory</a> held around 400,000 books salvaged by Tusi from the siege of Baghdad and other cities. Chinese astronomers brought by Hulagu Khan worked there as well.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Kublai_Khan" title="Kublai Khan">Kublai Khan</a> built a number of large observatories in China and his libraries included the <i>Wu-hu-lie-ti</i> (Euclid) brought by Muslim mathematicians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[128]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zhu_Shijie" title="Zhu Shijie">Zhu Shijie</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guo_Shoujing" title="Guo Shoujing">Guo Shoujing</a> were notable mathematicians in Yuan China. The Mongol physician <a href="/wiki/Hu_Sihui" title="Hu Sihui">Hu Sihui</a> described the importance of a healthy diet in a 1330 medical treatise.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ghazan_Khan" title="Ghazan Khan">Ghazan Khan</a>, able to understand four languages including Latin, built the Tabriz Observatory in 1295. The Byzantine Greek astronomer <a href="/wiki/Gregory_Chioniades" title="Gregory Chioniades">Gregory Chioniades</a> studied there under <a href="/wiki/Ajall_Shams_al-Din_Omar" title="Ajall Shams al-Din Omar">Ajall Shams al-Din Omar</a> who had worked at Maragheh under Tusi. Chioniades played an important role in transmitting several innovations from the Islamic world to Europe. These include the introduction of the universal latitude-independent astrolabe to Europe and a Greek description of the Tusi-couple, which would later have an influence on Copernican heliocentrism. Choniades also translated several Zij treatises into Greek, including the Persian Zij-i Ilkhani by al-Tusi and the Maragheh observatory. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine-Mongol_alliance" title="Byzantine-Mongol alliance">Byzantine-Mongol alliance</a> and the fact that the <a href="/wiki/Empire_of_Trebizond" title="Empire of Trebizond">Empire of Trebizond</a> was an <a href="/wiki/Ilkhanate" title="Ilkhanate">Ilkhanate</a> vassal facilitated Choniades' movements between Constantinople, Trebizond and Tabriz. Prince Radna, the Mongol viceroy of Tibet based in Gansu province, patronized the Samarkandi astronomer al-Sanjufini. The Arabic astronomical handbook dedicated by al-Sanjufini to Prince Radna, a descendant of Kublai Khan, was completed in 1363. It is notable for having <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_Mongolian" title="Middle Mongolian">Middle Mongolian</a> glosses on its margins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[129]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mail_system">Mail system</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Yam_(route)" title="Yam (route)">Yam (route)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:OljeituToPhilippeLeBel1305.jpg"><img alt="A partially unrolled scroll, opened from left to right to show a portion of the scroll with widely spaced vertical lines in the Mongol language. Imprinted over two of the lines is an official-looking square red stamp with an intricate design." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1519" data-file-width="1785" decoding="async" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/OljeituToPhilippeLeBel1305.jpg/220px-OljeituToPhilippeLeBel1305.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/OljeituToPhilippeLeBel1305.jpg/330px-OljeituToPhilippeLeBel1305.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/OljeituToPhilippeLeBel1305.jpg/440px-OljeituToPhilippeLeBel1305.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:OljeituToPhilippeLeBel1305.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A 1305 letter (on a scroll measuring <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1020198016">.mw-parser-output .frac{white-space:nowrap}.mw-parser-output .frac .num,.mw-parser-output .frac .den{font-size:80%;line-height:0;vertical-align:super}.mw-parser-output .frac .den{vertical-align:sub}.mw-parser-output .sr-only{border:0;clip:rect(0,0,0,0);height:1px;margin:-1px;overflow:hidden;padding:0;position:absolute;width:1px}</style>302 by 50 cm or 9 ft 11 in by 1 ft <span class="frac" role="math">7<span class="sr-only">+</span><span class="num">1</span>⁄<span class="den">2</span></span> in) from the Ilkhan Mongol <a href="/wiki/%C3%96ljait%C3%BC" title="Öljaitü">Öljaitü</a> to King <a href="/wiki/Philip_IV_of_France" title="Philip IV of France">Philip IV of France</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The Mongol Empire had an ingenious and efficient mail system for the time, often referred to by scholars as the <a href="/wiki/Yam_(route)" title="Yam (route)">Yam</a>. It had lavishly furnished and well-guarded relay posts known as <i>örtöö</i> set up throughout the Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chambers_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chambers-134">[130]</a></sup> A messenger would typically travel 40 kilometres (25 miles) from one station to the next, either receiving a fresh, rested horse, or relaying the mail to the next rider to ensure the speediest possible delivery. The Mongol riders regularly covered 200 km (125 mi) per day, better than the fastest record set by the <a href="/wiki/Pony_Express" title="Pony Express">Pony Express</a> some 600 years later.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The relay stations had attached households to service them. Anyone with a <a href="/wiki/Paiza" title="Paiza">paiza</a> was allowed to stop there for re-mounts and specified rations, while those carrying military identities used the Yam even without a paiza. Many merchants, messengers, and travelers from China, the Middle East, and Europe used the system. When the great khan died in Karakorum, news reached the Mongol forces under <a href="/wiki/Batu_Khan" title="Batu Khan">Batu Khan</a> in Central Europe within 4–6 weeks thanks to the Yam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weatherford._p._158_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Weatherford._p._158-59">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>Genghis and his successor <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%96gedei" title="Ögedei">Ögedei</a> built a wide system of roads, one of which carved through the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Altai_Range" title="Altai Range">Altai mountains</a>. After his enthronement, Ögedei further expanded the road system, ordering the Chagatai Khanate and Golden Horde to link up roads in western parts of the Mongol Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Kublai_Khan" title="Kublai Khan">Kublai Khan</a>, founder of the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a>, built special relays for high officials, as well as ordinary relays, that had hostels. During Kublai's reign, the Yuan communication system consisted of some 1,400 postal stations, which used 50,000 horses, 8,400 oxen, 6,700 mules, 4,000 carts, and 6,000 boats.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Manchuria" title="Manchuria">Manchuria</a> and southern <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, the Mongols still used <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dogsled" title="Dogsled">dogsled</a> relays for the Yam. In the Ilkhanate, Ghazan restored the declining relay system in the Middle East on a restricted scale. He constructed some hostels and decreed that only imperial envoys could receive a stipend. The Jochids of the Golden Horde financed their relay system by a special Yam tax.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Silk_Road">Silk Road</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Pax_Mongolica" title="Pax Mongolica">Pax Mongolica</a> and <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Toda_Mongke_and_His_Mongol_Horde.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5612" data-file-width="3683" decoding="async" height="259" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Toda_Mongke_and_His_Mongol_Horde.jpg/170px-Toda_Mongke_and_His_Mongol_Horde.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Toda_Mongke_and_His_Mongol_Horde.jpg/255px-Toda_Mongke_and_His_Mongol_Horde.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Toda_Mongke_and_His_Mongol_Horde.jpg/340px-Toda_Mongke_and_His_Mongol_Horde.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Toda_Mongke_and_His_Mongol_Horde.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tuda_Mengu" title="Tuda Mengu">Tuda Mengu</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Horde" title="Golden Horde">Golden Horde</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The Mongols had a history of supporting merchants and trade. Genghis Khan had encouraged foreign merchants early in his career, even before uniting the Mongols. Merchants provided information about neighboring cultures, served as diplomats and official traders for the Mongols, and were essential for many goods, since the Mongols produced little of their own.
</p><p>Mongol government and elites provided capital for merchants and sent them far afield, in an <i>ortoq</i> (merchant partner) arrangement. In Mongol times, the contractual features of a Mongol-<i>ortoq</i> partnership closely resembled that of <a href="/wiki/Qirad" title="Qirad">qirad</a> and commenda arrangements, however, Mongol investors were not constrained using uncoined precious metals and tradable goods for partnership investments and primarily financed money-lending and trade activities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[132]</a></sup> Moreover, Mongol elites formed trade partnerships with merchants from Italian cities, including <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a>’s family.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[133]</a></sup> As the empire grew, any merchants or ambassadors with proper documentation and authorization received protection and sanctuary as they traveled through Mongol realms. Well-traveled and relatively well-maintained roads linked lands from the Mediterranean basin to China, greatly increasing overland trade and resulting in some dramatic stories of those who travelled through what would become known as the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a>.
</p><p>Western explorer <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a> traveled east along the Silk Road, and the Chinese Mongol monk <a href="/wiki/Rabban_Bar_Sauma" title="Rabban Bar Sauma">Rabban Bar Sauma</a> made a comparably epic journey along the route, venturing from his home of <a href="/wiki/Khanbaliq" title="Khanbaliq">Khanbaliq</a> (Beijing) as far as Europe. European missionaries, such as <a href="/wiki/William_of_Rubruck" title="William of Rubruck">William of Rubruck</a>, also traveled to the Mongol court to convert believers to their cause, or went as papal envoys to correspond with Mongol rulers in an attempt to secure a <a href="/wiki/Franco-Mongol_alliance" title="Franco-Mongol alliance">Franco-Mongol alliance</a>. It was rare, however, for anyone to journey the full length of Silk Road. Instead, merchants moved products like a bucket brigade, goods being traded from one middleman to another, moving from China all the way to the West; the goods moved over such long distances fetched extravagant prices.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gold_coin_of_Genghis_Khan,_struck_at_the_Ghazna_(Ghazni)_mint.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="384" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="106" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Gold_coin_of_Genghis_Khan%2C_struck_at_the_Ghazna_%28Ghazni%29_mint.jpg/220px-Gold_coin_of_Genghis_Khan%2C_struck_at_the_Ghazna_%28Ghazni%29_mint.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Gold_coin_of_Genghis_Khan%2C_struck_at_the_Ghazna_%28Ghazni%29_mint.jpg/330px-Gold_coin_of_Genghis_Khan%2C_struck_at_the_Ghazna_%28Ghazni%29_mint.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Gold_coin_of_Genghis_Khan%2C_struck_at_the_Ghazna_%28Ghazni%29_mint.jpg/440px-Gold_coin_of_Genghis_Khan%2C_struck_at_the_Ghazna_%28Ghazni%29_mint.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gold_coin_of_Genghis_Khan,_struck_at_the_Ghazna_(Ghazni)_mint.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gold <i><a href="/wiki/Gold_dinar" title="Gold dinar">dinar</a></i> of Genghis Khan, struck at the <a href="/wiki/Ghazni" title="Ghazni">Ghazna</a> (Ghazni) mint, dated 1221/2</div></div></div>
<p>After Genghis, the merchant partner business continued to flourish under his successors Ögedei and Güyük. Merchants brought clothing, food, information, and other provisions to the imperial palaces, and in return the great khans gave the merchants tax exemptions and allowed them to use the official relay stations of the Mongol Empire. Merchants also served as tax farmers in China, Russia and Iran. If the merchants were attacked by bandits, losses were made up from the imperial treasury.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Policies changed under the Great Khan <a href="/wiki/M%C3%B6ngke_Khan" title="Möngke Khan">Möngke</a>. Because of money laundering and overtaxing, he attempted to limit abuses and sent imperial investigators to supervise the <i>ortoq</i> businesses. He decreed that all merchants must pay commercial and property taxes, and he paid off all drafts drawn by high-ranking Mongol elites from the merchants. This policy continued under the Yuan dynasty.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>The fall of the Mongol Empire in the 14th century led to the collapse of the political, cultural, and economic unity along the Silk Road. Turkic tribes seized the western end of the route from the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>, sowing the seeds of a Turkic culture that would later crystallize into the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a> under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sunni" title="Sunni">Sunni</a> faith. In the East, the Han Chinese overthrew the Yuan dynasty in 1368, launching their own <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a> and pursuing a policy of economic isolationism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-footsteps_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-footsteps-138">[134]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Mongolia" title="History of Mongolia">History of Mongolia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mongols-map.png"><img alt="Map of Asia" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="525" data-file-width="728" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Mongols-map.png/220px-Mongols-map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Mongols-map.png/330px-Mongols-map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Mongols-map.png/440px-Mongols-map.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mongols-map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map showing the boundary of the 13th century Mongol Empire compared to today's Mongols in <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a>, Russia, the <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asian</a> States, and China</div></div></div>
<p>The Mongol Empire, at its height of the largest contiguous empire in history, had a lasting impact, unifying large regions. Some of these (such as eastern and western Russia, and the western parts of China) remain unified today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[135]</a></sup> Mongols might have been assimilated into local populations after the fall of the empire, and some of their descendants adopted local <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religions</a>. For example, the eastern khanate largely adopted <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, and the three western khanates adopted <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, largely under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sufi" title="Sufi">Sufi</a> influence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foltz._pp._105–106_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-Foltz._pp._105–106-125">[121]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to some<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="Statement needs to be more specific about the content to which it refers. (February 2012)">specify</span></a></i>]</sup> interpretations, Genghis Khan's conquests caused <a href="/wiki/Destruction_under_the_Mongol_Empire" title="Destruction under the Mongol Empire">wholesale destruction on an unprecedented scale</a> in certain geographic regions, leading to changes in the demographics of Asia.
</p><p>The non-military achievements of the Mongol Empire include the introduction of a writing system, a Mongol alphabet based on the characters of the <a href="/wiki/Old_Uyghur" title="Old Uyghur">Old Uyghur</a>, which is still used in <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a> today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[136]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Facial_Chronicle_-_b.10,_p.049_-_Tokhtamysh_at_Moscow.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="796" data-file-width="438" decoding="async" height="309" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Facial_Chronicle_-_b.10%2C_p.049_-_Tokhtamysh_at_Moscow.jpg/170px-Facial_Chronicle_-_b.10%2C_p.049_-_Tokhtamysh_at_Moscow.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Facial_Chronicle_-_b.10%2C_p.049_-_Tokhtamysh_at_Moscow.jpg/255px-Facial_Chronicle_-_b.10%2C_p.049_-_Tokhtamysh_at_Moscow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Facial_Chronicle_-_b.10%2C_p.049_-_Tokhtamysh_at_Moscow.jpg/340px-Facial_Chronicle_-_b.10%2C_p.049_-_Tokhtamysh_at_Moscow.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Facial_Chronicle_-_b.10,_p.049_-_Tokhtamysh_at_Moscow.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Tokhtamysh" title="Tokhtamysh">Tokhtamysh</a> and the armies of the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Horde" title="Golden Horde">Golden Horde</a> initiate the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Moscow_(1382)" title="Siege of Moscow (1382)">Siege of Moscow (1382)</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Some of the other long-term consequences of the Mongol Empire include:
</p>
<ul><li>Moscow rose to prominence while it was still under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timeline_of_the_Tataro-Mongol_Yoke_in_Russia" title="Timeline of the Tataro-Mongol Yoke in Russia">rule of the Mongol-Tatar yoke</a>, some time after Russian rulers were accorded the status of tax collectors for the Mongols. The fact that the Russians collected tribute and taxes for the Mongols meant that the Mongols themselves rarely visited the lands which they owned. The Russians eventually gained military power, and their ruler <a href="/wiki/Ivan_III_of_Russia" title="Ivan III of Russia">Ivan III</a> completely overthrew the Mongols and formed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moscow_principality" title="Moscow principality">Russian Tsardom</a>. After the <a href="/wiki/Great_Stand_on_the_Ugra_River" title="Great Stand on the Ugra River">Great Stand on the Ugra River</a> proved that the Mongols were vulnerable, the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Moscow" title="Grand Duchy of Moscow">Grand Duchy of Moscow</a> gained independence.</li>
<li>Europe's knowledge of the known world was immensely expanded by the information which was brought back to it by ambassadors and merchants. When <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">Columbus</a> sailed in 1492, his mission was to reach <a href="/wiki/Cathay" title="Cathay">Cathay</a>, the land of the Grand Khan in China, and give him a letter from the monarchs <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_II_of_Aragon" title="Ferdinand II of Aragon">Ferdinand II of Aragon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isabella_I_of_Castile" title="Isabella I of Castile">Isabella I of Castile</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></li>
<li>Some studies indicate that the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a> which devastated Europe in the late 1340s may have traveled from China to Europe along the trade routes of the Mongol Empire.
